A Pillar Must Exist Regardless of Its Manner
Explains the principle that a pillar is valid regardless of the performer's state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle regarding pillars (arkan) and apply it to determine the validity of wuquf at Arafah and fasting when the performer is unconscious or unaware.

Core Idea
A pillar (rukn) is valid simply by its existence; the state of the performer does not matter.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: a pillar of worship only requires its physical occurrence, regardless of the performer's consciousness or awareness. The author of al-Hidayah uses wuquf at Arafah as the primary example, showing that even if a pilgrim passes through while asleep or unconscious, the wuquf is valid. The same logic applies to fasting – sleeping all day does not break the fast because the pillar (abstention) continues to exist. Understanding this principle clarifies many rules about the validity of ibadat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The text states: 'ما هو ركن لا بد من وجوده كيفما كان' – a pillar must exist regardless of how. Understand that this principle prioritizes the existence of the act over the condition of the performer.
This is a general principle, not limited to Hajj.
2
Apply to wuquf at Arafah
The text gives three scenarios: passing through asleep, unconscious, or not knowing it is Arafah. In each case, wuquf is valid. Confirm that the pillar (presence in Arafah) has occurred.
Intention is not a condition for this pillar.
3
Apply to fasting (sawm)
The text analogizes to fasting: if a person intends to fast at suhur and then sleeps all day, the fast is valid because the pillar (abstention) exists throughout.
The intention is only required at the beginning, not for the continuation.
4
Identify the scope and exception
Note that intention (niyyah) is not a condition for every pillar; it is required for some acts (e.g., wudu) but not for wuquf or the fasting day. The principle emphasizes that the pillar itself is the essence.
This is a Hanafi distinctive view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مَا هُوَ رُكُنْ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ كَيْفَمَا كَانَ. ترجمہ : جوشی رکن ہو اس کا موجود ہونا ضروری ہے جس طرح بھی ہو۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو چیز رکن ہو اس کا صرف پایا جانا ضروری ہے،اب اس کا پایا جانا جس طرح بھی ہو ، جس حالت میں بھی ہو۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر حاجی صاحب عرفہ سے آگے نکل گیا نیند کی حالت میں، یا بے ہوشی کی حالت میں ، یا حاجی کو یہ پتہ نہیں تھا کہ یہ میدانِ عرفہ ہے، تو حاجی کے وقوف سے یہ کفایت کرے گا، یعنی اس کا وقوف عرفہ ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ وقوف عرفہ رکن ہے اور جو چیز رکن ہو اس کا موجود ہونا صرف ضروری ہے ، جس طرح بھی ہو اس شخص کا وقوف عرفہ پایا گیا ہے۔ مرور کی صورت میں بھی حج کا رکن یعنی وقوف پایا گیا اور اعماء ونوم وقوف کے منافی نہیں ہیں، جیسے اگر کسی شخص نے روزے کی نیت سے سحری کی اور پھر پورے دن سویا رہا، یا بے ہوش پڑا رہا تو رکن صوم یعنی امساک کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے اس شخص کا روز ہ اداء ہو جائے گا، اسی طرح رکن حج یعنی وقوف کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے حج اداء ہو جائے گا۔ وَمَنْ اجْتَازَ بِعَرَفَةَ وَهُوَ نَائِمٌ أَوْ مُغْمِّى عَلَيْهِ أَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّهَا عَرَفَةً أَجْزَأَهُ ذَلِكَ عَنْ الْوُقُوفِ وَهَذَا إِذَا أَحْرَمَ وَهُوَ مُفِيقٌ ثُمَّ أُغْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ حَالَ الْوُقُوفِ فَإِنَّهُ يُجْزِتُهُ الْوُقُوفُ إِجْمَاعًا لأَنَّ مَا هُوَ الرُّكْـنُ قَدْ وُجِدَ وَهُوَ الْوُقُولُ فَلَا يَمْنَعُهُ الْإِغْمَاءِ وَالنَّوْمُ كَرُكُنِ الصَّوْمِ وَإِنَّمَا اخْتَلَّ مِنْهُ النِّيَّة وَهِيَ لَيْسَتْ بِشَرْطِ لِكُلِّ رُكُن. [الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الحج، باب الإحرام ج ا ص ۱۲۱، ط: المطبعة الخيرية] الهداية كتاب الحج، باب الإحرام، فصل ، ج ۱ ص ۲۷۷ ،۲، ط: رحمانیہ
Indeed, what is a pillar, its existence is necessary however it may be. Translation: Whatever is a pillar, its existence is necessary in whatever manner it occurs. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that a thing that is a pillar only needs to be found, no matter how it is found, in whatever state. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, explains that if a pilgrim passes by Arafah while asleep, or in a coma, or does not know that it is Arafah, that suffices him for wuquf, meaning his wuquf becomes valid. Because wuquf at Arafah is a pillar, and a thing that is a pillar only requires its existence; in whatever manner, this person's wuquf at Arafah has occurred. Even in passing, the pillar of Hajj, i.e., wuquf, has occurred, and unconsciousness and sleep do not contradict wuquf. Just as if a person intends fasting at suhur and then sleeps all day or lies unconscious, then the pillar of fasting, i.e., abstention (imsak), is found, so his fast is valid. Similarly, due to the occurrence of the pillar of Hajj, i.e., wuquf, the Hajj is valid. And whoever passes through Arafah while asleep, or unconscious, or does not know that it is Arafah, that suffices him for wuquf. This is when he entered ihram while conscious, then became unconscious at the time of wuquf; then wuquf suffices him by consensus, because the pillar, which is presence (wusul), has occurred. Unconsciousness and sleep do not prevent it, like the pillar of fasting. The only thing lacking is intention (niyyah), and intention is not a condition for every pillar. (Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Ihram vol.1 p.121, al-Matba'ah al-Khayriyya) (Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Ihram, Fasl, vol.1 p.277, 2, Rahmania)
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi principle regarding pillars (arkan) in worship: the existence of the pillar itself is sufficient, regardless of the performer's state (awake, asleep, unconscious, or ignorant). The author applies this to wuquf at Arafah, the central pillar of Hajj. Even if a person passes through Arafah asleep or unconscious, the wuquf is valid because the requirement is simply to be present in Arafah at the correct time. The state of consciousness does not negate the pillar, just as in fasting, sleeping all day does not break the fast—the pillar of abstaining from food, drink, and sexual relations remains valid. The quote from Al-Jawhara and Al-Hidayah reaffirms this by stating that wuquf suffices by consensus when one is unconscious, drawing an analogy to the pillar of fasting. The only missing element is intention (niyyah), but that is not a condition for every pillar; for wuquf, being present is the essence.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): An essential component of an act of worship; its absence invalidates the act.
وقوف عرفة (wuquf ‘Arafah): The act of being present at Arafah at the prescribed time; the primary pillar of Hajj.
إمساك (imsak): Abstaining from eating, drinking, and sexual activity during fasting; the pillar of sawm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a pilgrim passes through Arafah while unconscious due to illness, what is the ruling on his Hajj?
A) His Hajj is invalid because he lacked intention
B) His wuquf is valid and Hajj is complete
C) He must make up the wuquf the next day
D) He must sacrifice an animal as compensation

Key Takeaway
The existence of a pillar (rukn) in worship suffices regardless of the performer's state; intention and consciousness are not required for every pillar.

Initiating Ihram by Action: The Principle of Qilada
Establishes that ihram can be entered by an action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that ihram can be entered by an action (placing a qilada on a hady) and apply it to determine when a person becomes a muhrim.

Core Idea
Entering ihram does not require words; a physical act like adorning a sacrificial animal can suffice.

In the Hanafi school, the state of ihram—the sacred state for Hajj—is typically entered by reciting the talbiyah. However, this passage from Al-Hidayah introduces a broader principle: the response to Allah's call (ijabah) can be expressed by action as well as speech. Specifically, if a person places a qilada (a necklace) on his sacrificial animal and proceeds with the intention of Hajj, he becomes a muhrim even without uttering talbiyah. This lesson unpacks that principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its significance
The act is placing a qilada (necklace) on a sacrificial animal (hady). This is an outward sign of dedicating the animal for Hajj. According to the passage, this action constitutes an expression of ijabah (response to Allah's command).
Ijabah here means the response to the call of Ibrahim to perform Hajj.
2
Understand the principle
The principle states: 'Indeed, the expression of response (ijabah) may be by action just as it is by speech.' This means that entering the state of ihram is not confined to verbal talbiyah; any clear physical act that demonstrates the intention to perform Hajj can establish ihram.
The Hanafi school extends this principle to other acts, like wearing a special garment or marking an animal.
3
Apply the principle to determine status
If a person ties a qilada on a hady with the intention of Hajj and then proceeds toward Makkah, he is considered a muhrim from that moment, even if he never recites talbiyah. All prohibitions of ihram apply.
The key is the combination of intention and a physical manifestation.
4
Note the madhhab difference (implied)
The Shafi'i school requires verbal talbiyah to enter ihram and does not accept an action alone. This principle highlights a key difference in how each school defines the expression of ihram.
This difference arises from varying interpretations of the hadith on talbiyah.
5
Examine the supporting quote
The passage includes a quote about wuquf at Arafah while unconscious to show that intention (niyyah) is not a condition for every pillar. Similarly, for ihram, the physical act suffices even if no verbal declaration occurs.
This reinforces the idea that the essence of ihram is the outward expression, not the verbal utterance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢٠) إِنَّ إِظْهَارَ الْإِجَابَةِ قَدْ يَكُونُ بِالْفِعْل كَمَا يَكُونُ بِالْقَوْل . ترجمہ: اجابت کا اظہار جس طرح قول سے ہوتا ہے اسی طرح کبھی فعل سے بھی ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جس طرح اجابت قول یعنی تلبیہ کے ساتھ ظاہر ہوتی ہے اسی طرح کبھی کسی فعل یعنی ہدی کے گلے میں قلادہ ڈالنے سے بھی ظاہر ہوتی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ جس نے بد نہ یعنی ہدی کے جانور کا قلادہ باندھا چا ہے وہ بد نہ نکلی ہو، یا نذر کی وجہ سے ہو ، یا کسی پرندے کے شکار کا بدلہ ہو، اور خود بھی وہ آدمی اس ہدی مقلدہ کے ساتھ متوجہ ہوا اور حج کا ارادہ بھی کیا تو یہ شخص محرم شمار ہو گا اور محرم کے جتنے احکام اور محذورات احترام ہیں وہ اس پر لاگو ہوں گے اگر چہ تلبیہ نہ پڑھے، کیونکہ تلبیہ سے مقصود (قَوْلُهُ وَمَنْ اجْتَازَ بِعَرَفَةَ وَهُوَ نَائِمٌ أَوْ مُغْمِّى عَلَيْهِ أَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّهَا عَرَفَةً أَجْزَأَهُ ذَلِكَ عَنْ الْوُقُوفِ وَهَذَا إِذَا أَحْرَمَ وَهُوَ مُفِيقٌ ثُمَّ أُغْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ حَالَ الْوُقُوفِ فَإِنَّهُ يُجْزِتُهُ الْوُقُوفُ إِجْمَاعًا لأَنَّ مَا هُوَ الرُّكْـنُ قَدْ وُجِدَ وَهُوَ الْوُقُولُ فَلَا يَمْنَعُهُ الْإِغْمَاءِ وَالنَّوْمُ كَرُكُنِ الصَّوْمِ وَإِنَّمَا اخْتَلَّ مِنْهُ النِّيَّة وَهِيَ لَيْسَتْ بِشَرْطِ لِكُلِّ رُكُن. [الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الحج، باب الإحرام، ج ۱ ص ۱۶۲، ط: المطبعة الخيرية] الهداية كتاب الحج، باب الإحرام فصل، ج ۱ ص ۲۷۸، ط: رحمانیہ
(20) Indeed, the expression of response (ijabah) may be by action just as it is by speech. Translation: The expression of response becomes apparent by speech sometimes and by action sometimes. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that just as response is shown by speech, i.e., talbiyah, similarly it is sometimes shown by an action, i.e., placing a qilada (necklace) around the neck of a hady (sacrificial animal). The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, explains that a person who ties a qilada on a badanah (sacrificial animal) – whether it is a voluntary offering, due to a vow, or as compensation for hunting a bird – and then he himself proceeds with that muqalladah (animal adorned with qilada) and also intends Hajj, then that person will be considered a muhrim (in ihram), and all the rulings and prohibitions of ihram apply to him, even if he did not recite the talbiyah. Because the purpose of talbiyah is (his statement: And whoever passes through Arafah while asleep, or unconscious, or does not know that it is Arafah, that suffices him for wuquf... etc.) [Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Ihram, vol.1 p.162, al-Matba'ah al-Khayriyya] [Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Ihram, Fasl, vol.1 p.278, Rahmania]
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage introduces a principle that entering the state of ihram can be initiated not only by the verbal declaration of talbiyah but also by a physical action – namely, placing a qilada (a necklace or collar) on a sacrificial animal (hady) and then proceeding with it with the intention of Hajj. According to the Hanafi school, a person who does this becomes a muhrim even without uttering talbiyah. This is because the action of preparing the animal and heading to the Sacred Mosque with that intent is a clear expression of responding to the call of Hajj (ijabah). The author then cites a long quote from Al-Jawhara and Al-Hidayah regarding wuquf at Arafah while unconscious, which seems to be included to show that intention (niyyah) is not a condition for every pillar – tying the principle back to the previous lesson. The key point is that the physical act of marking the animal signifies entering ihram, and all prohibitions of ihram (like wearing sewn clothes, applying perfume, etc.) apply from that moment.

Key Terms
إظهار الإجابة (izhar al-ijabah): The outward manifestation of accepting the call to Hajj (response to Allah's command), typically by talbiyah or by a physical sign.
قلادہ (qiladah): A necklace or collar placed on a sacrificial animal (hady) to mark it as an offering for Hajj.
تلبيہ (talbiyah): The verbal proclamation 'Labbaik Allahumma labbaik...' that a pilgrim recites to enter ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is not in ihram until he recites talbiyah
B) He is in ihram and must observe ihram prohibitions
C) He must repeat the process with talbiyah
D) His Hajj is invalid because talbiyah is obligatory

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, entering ihram can be achieved by a physical action like placing a qilada on a hady, not only by verbal talbiyah.

Validity of Wuquf Despite Lack of Consciousness or Awareness
States that wuquf at Arafah is valid even if one is asleep, unconscious, or unaware

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wuquf for an unconscious pilgrim and explain the qiyas to fasting that supports it.

Core Idea
Wuquf is valid even when unconscious because the rukn is presence, not intention.

A common concern among pilgrims is whether their wuquf at Arafah is valid if they fall asleep or become unconscious. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling: the pillar of wuquf is simply being present, regardless of one's state of awareness. The proof comes from consensus (ijma) and analogy (qiyas) to fasting, where the pillar (abstention) is fulfilled even if one sleeps the whole day.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the opening statement: whoever passes through Arafah while asleep, unconscious, or unaware, it suffices. This is the ruling for wuquf.
The ruling applies to the essential pillar (rukn) of Hajj.
2
Understand the consensus (ijma) basis
The text says 'by consensus' (ijma'an) that if one was conscious at ihram and then became unconscious at the time of wuquf, the wuquf is valid. This shows communal agreement.
Ijma' is a primary source of law in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the qiyas to fasting
The author draws an analogy: just as fasting is valid even if one sleeps through the day because the pillar (imsak) is present, so too wuquf is valid because the pillar (presence) is present. Identify the asl (fasting), far' (wuquf), and illa (fulfillment of the rukn).
This is a jali (clear) qiyas with a straightforward illa.
4
Note the distinction about intention
The text explicitly states that intention (niyyah) is not a condition for every pillar. The rukn is valid by its mere existence, even without conscious intent.
This clarifies a potential misconception: intention is required for some acts but not for the rukn of wuquf.
5
Apply to scenarios
Consider cases: a pilgrim who sleeps through the entire wuquf time, a pilgrim who has a heatstroke and becomes unconscious, or a pilgrim who passes through Arafah without knowing it. In all these, the wuquf is valid according to this ruling.
The key is physical presence within the boundaries of Arafah at the appointed time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ اجْتَازَ بِعَرَفَةَ وَهُوَ نَائِمٌ أَوْ مُغْمِّى عَلَيْهِ أَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّهَا عَرَفَةً أَجْزَأَهُ ذَلِكَ عَنْ الْوُقُوفِ وَهَذَا إِذَا أَحْرَمَ وَهُوَ مُفِيقٌ ثُمَّ أُغْمِيَ عَلَيْهِ حَالَ الْوُقُوفِ فَإِنَّهُ يُجْزِتُهُ الْوُقُوفُ إِجْمَاعًا لأَنَّ مَا هُوَ الرُّكْـنُ قَدْ وُجِدَ وَهُوَ الْوُقُولُ فَلَا يَمْنَعُهُ الْإِغْمَاءِ وَالنَّوْمُ كَرُكُنِ الصَّوْمِ وَإِنَّمَا اخْتَلَّ مِنْهُ النِّيَّة وَهِيَ لَيْسَتْ بِشَرْطِ لِكُلِّ رُكُن.
And whoever passes through Arafah while he is asleep, or unconscious, or does not know that it is Arafah, that suffices him for the standing. And this is when he assumed ihram while conscious, then he became unconscious at the time of standing, then the standing suffices him by consensus because the pillar has been found, which is the presence, so unconsciousness and sleep do not prevent it, like the pillar of fasting. And what is missing from him is the intention, and it is not a condition for every pillar.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah. It establishes that the essential pillar (rukn) of wuquf at Arafah is simply being present in the area, regardless of one's state of consciousness or knowledge. The proof is by analogy (qiyas) to fasting: just as the rukn of fasting is abstention (imsak), which is present even if one sleeps through the day, so too does the rukn of wuquf (being present) suffice even if one is asleep, unconscious, or unaware. The text explicitly states that intention (niyyah) is not a condition for the rukn; the pillar is valid by its mere existence. The ruling is by consensus (ijma'). The source is al-Hidayah, which relies on the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
عرفة (Arafah): The plain near Makkah where pilgrims stand on the 9th of Dhul-Hijjah.
وقوف (wuquf): Standing or being present at Arafah during Hajj.
ركن (rukn): An integral part of an act of worship without which the act is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that wuquf at Arafah is purely a matter of presence; consciousness and intention are not conditions for its validity.

Declaration of Ihram Through Action: Garlanding a Sacrificial Animal
Establishes that ihram can be declared through action (garlanding) as well as speech (talbiyah).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that garlanding a sacrificial animal with intention creates ihram and explain the reasoning (ta'lil) that it is a distinctive ritual of Hajj expressing ijabah.

Core Idea
Garlanding a sacrificial animal with intention creates ihram, because action can express acceptance as effectively as speech.

The Hanafi school teaches that entering the state of ihram can be accomplished not only by verbal talbiyah but also by actions uniquely associated with Hajj. This lesson explores the ruling that garlanding (taqlid) a sacrificial animal, combined with the intention of Hajj or Umrah, establishes ihram. The reasoning is grounded in the concept of ijabah (acceptance of Allah's call), which can be expressed physically as well as verbally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize the specific act: garlanding a sacrificial animal (taqlid al-hady) while intending Hajj or Umrah. The ruling is that this makes the person a muhrim (in ihram), even without reciting talbiyah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof in the passage: the reasoning (ta'lil) states that garlanding is a distinctive ritual (sha'irah) of Hajj, akin to talbiyah, and it expresses ijabah.
The dalil is a qiyas (analogy) based on the shared illa of expressing ijabah through a Hajj ritual.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the dalil proves the ruling: since talbiyah creates ihram due to expressing ijabah, and garlanding also expresses ijabah through a Hajj ritual, the same ruling applies.
The effective cause (illa) is the expression of ijabah, which is present in both acts.
4
Confirm the Hanafi position
Note that this is the established position in Zahir al-Riwayah, with no significant disagreement among the major Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
The ruling is sahih (valid) and mufta bihi.
5
Note the practical consequence
Understand that a person who garlands an animal with intention must observe all ihram prohibitions, even without uttering talbiyah.
This widens the means of entering ihram for those who may be unable to speak or prefer action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَانَّ سَوْقَ الْهَدْيِ بَعْدَ التَّقْلِيدِ فِي مَعْنَى التَّلْبِيَةِ إِذْ لَا يَفْعَلُ ذَلِكَ إِلَّا مَنْ يُرِيدُ الْحَجَّ أَوْ الْعُمْرَةَ فَصَارَ مِنْ خَصَائِصِهِ كَالتَّلْبِيَةِ إِذْ الْمَقْصُودُ بِالتَّلْبِيَّةِ إِظْهَارُ الْإِجَابَةِ لِلدَّعْوَةِ وَبِتَقْلِيدِ الْهَدَى يَحْصُلُ إِظْهَارُ الْإِجَابَةِ أَيْضًا وَإِظْهَارُ الْإِجَابَةِ قَدْ يَكُونُ بِالْفِعْلِ كَمَا يَكُونُ بِالْقَوْلِ وَهَذَا، لِأَنَّ التَّقْلِيدَ مِنْ شَعَائِرِ الْحَجِّ كَالتَّلْبِيَةِ، فَإِذَا اتَّصَلَ بِالنِّيَّةِ يَكُونُ مُحْرِمًا كَالتَّلْبِيَةِ.
And because driving the sacrificial animal after garlanding it is in the sense of talbiyah, since no one does that except one who intends Hajj or Umrah, so it becomes one of its distinctive features like talbiyah. Since the purpose of talbiyah is to express acceptance of the call, and by garlanding the sacrificial animal, expressing acceptance is also achieved. And expressing acceptance may be by action just as it is by speech. This is because garlanding is among the rituals of Hajj like talbiyah. So if it is connected to intention, one becomes a muhrim just as with talbiyah.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Tabyin al-Haqaiq, explaining the principle that acceptance (ijabah) can be shown by action as well as speech. The specific ruling is that if a person garlands a sacrificial animal (taqlid) and sends it off with the intention of Hajj or Umrah, he becomes a muhrim (in ihram) even without reciting talbiyah. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that garlanding is a distinguishing ritual (sha'irah) of Hajj, similar to talbiyah, and it expresses acceptance of Allah's call. The text emphasizes that the act combined with intention suffices to establish ihram. This is based on the general principle that ijabah can be by action. The source is from al-Hidayah and Tabyin al-Haqaiq, which are part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
تلبیہ (talbiyah): The verbal declaration 'Labbaik Allahumma labbaik' recited by the pilgrim to express intention for Hajj/Umrah.
قلادہ (qiladah): A garland placed around the neck of a sacrificial animal to mark it for Hajj.
اجابت (ijabah): Acceptance or response to Allah's call; the purpose of talbiyah and garlanding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

No Overlap in Intended Acts of Worship: Qiran Requires Separate Acts
States the Hanafi principle of no overlap and applies it to qiran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of no overlap in intended acts of worship and apply it to explain why a qarin must perform separate tawaf and sa'y.

Core Idea
Intended acts of worship cannot be combined; each requires its own performance.

The Hanafi school establishes a fundamental rule: acts of worship that are intended for their own sake (maqsudah) cannot overlap. This means one cannot fulfill two distinct obligations with a single ritual action. In this lesson, we explore this principle through the case of qiran—performing Hajj and Umrah together—and see how it leads to a clear difference with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle and Its Scope
State the principle 'لا تداخل في العبادات المقصودة'. Understand that an intended act of worship is one performed for its own sake, not as a means to something else.
Worship acts that are ghayr maqsudah (like wudu) may overlap, but maqsudah acts may not.
2
Apply the Principle to Qiran
Recognize that both Hajj and Umrah are ibadat maqsudah. Therefore, each must be performed independently.
The qarin has one ihram for both, but the rituals must be separate.
3
Derive the Ruling for Tawaf and Sa'y
Since no overlap is allowed, the qarin must perform separate tawaf and sa'y for Hajj and for Umrah.
This is considered wajib in the Hanafi school.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i View
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that one tawaf and sa'y suffices for both because the journey is one. Contrast this with the Hanafi reliance on the principle.
The difference stems from differing views on whether acts of worship can be combined.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
In practice, a qarin following the Hanafi school must perform two tawafs and two sa'ys, while a Shafi'i qarin performs only one of each.
This affects planning and the number of rituals performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا تَدَاخُلَ فِي الْعِبَادَاتِ الْمَقْصُودَةِ.
There is no overlap in intended acts of worship.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the qiran form of Hajj (combining Hajj and Umrah with one ihram). The Hanafi school holds that since both Hajj and Umrah are intended acts of worship (ibadat maqsudah), their rituals, such as tawaf and sa'y, must be performed separately for each. The principle 'no overlap in intended acts of worship' prevents combining them into one performance. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, allowing one tawaf and sa'y to suffice for both. The Hanafi proof is this principle, citing that actions of worship that are independently intended (like Zhuhr and Asr prayers) cannot be combined in a single performance. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a qarin must perform two tawafs and two sa'ys according to Hanafis, while Shafi'is require only one. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): The legal concept of one act of worship sufficing for another, i.e., overlap.
عبادات مقصودہ (ibadat maqsudah): Acts of worship that are intended for their own sake, not merely as means to another end.
قارن (qarin): A person who performs Hajj and Umrah together with one ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Two tawafs: one for Umrah and one for Hajj.
C) Three tawafs: one for each and one extra.
D) None; tawaf is not required for qiran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly prohibits overlap in intended acts of worship, requiring separate performances for each, as exemplified by the qarin's obligation to perform two tawafs and two sa'ys.

The Principle of Non-Intermingling in Worship: Application to Qiran
States a principle and applies it to Qiran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of non-intermingling in intended acts of worship and derive the ruling for the Qarin using the evidence provided (companion narrations and qiyas).

Core Idea
No tadakhul in intended worship: Hajj and Umrah are separate acts requiring separate tawafs and sa'ys.

Classical Hanafi texts establish a key principle: acts of worship that are intended for their own sake cannot be merged into a single performance. This lesson examines that principle and its application to the Qarin—someone who performs Hajj and Umrah in one Ihram. We will explore the textual and analogical proofs that require the Qarin to perform two complete sets of tawaf and sa'y.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the principle 'There is no intermingling (tadakhul) in intended acts of worship'. Understand what 'intended acts' means: acts done for their own sake, like Hajj, Umrah, Salah, and fasting.
Distinguish from means or preparatory acts.
2
Analyze the First Proof: Talbiyah and Garlanding Analogy
Examine how the author links the garlanding (taqlid) of the sacrificial animal to talbiyah: both express intention and are characteristics of Ihram. This establishes that both Hajj and Umrah commence separately.
Both are actions that signify the intention to perform a specific worship.
3
Analyze the Second Proof: Companion Narrations
Look at the narration from al-Tahawi and Sa'id ibn Mansur transmitted by four Companions: 'Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, and 'Imran ibn al-Husayn. They explicitly state that the Qarin performs two tawafs and two sa'ys.
This is a direct textual evidence (qawl sahabi).
4
Evaluate the Qiyas Argument
Understand the analogy to Salah and Sawm: just as you cannot combine two prayers or two fasts into one act, you cannot combine Hajj and Umrah. The common illa (effective cause) is that they are distinct intended acts of worship.
This reasoning supports the prohibition of tadakhul.
5
Apply to the Qarin
Conclude that the Qarin must perform two separate tawafs and two separate sa'ys—one set for Hajj, one for Umrah. This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.
Any shortfall means the obligation is not fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا تَدَاخُلَ فِي الْعِبَادَاتِ الْمَقْصُودَةِ. وَلَانَّ سَوْقَ الْهَدْيِ بَعْدَ التَّقْلِيدِ فِي مَعْنَى التَّلْبِيَةِ إِذْ لَا يَفْعَلُ ذَلِكَ إِلَّا مَنْ يُرِيدُ الْحَجَّ أَوْ الْعُمْرَةَ فَصَارَ مِنْ خَصَائِصِهِ كَالتَّلْبِيَةِ إِذْ الْمَقْصُودُ بِالتَّلْبِيَّةِ إِظْهَارُ الْإِجَابَةِ لِلدَّعْوَةِ وَبِتَقْلِيدِ الْهَدَى يَحْصُلُ إِظْهَارُ الْإِجَابَةِ أَيْضًا وَإِظْهَارُ الْإِجَابَةِ قَدْ يَكُونُ بِالْفِعْلِ كَمَا يَكُونُ بِالْقَوْلِ وَهَذَا، لِأَنَّ التَّقْلِيدَ مِنْ شَعَائِرِ الْحَجِّ كَالتَّلْبِيَةِ، فَإِذَا اتَّصَلَ بِالنِّيَّةِ يَكُونُ مُحْرِمًا كَالتَّلْبِيَةِ. وَرَوَى الطَّحَاوِيُّ وَسَعِيدُ بْنُ مَنْصُورٍ عَنْ عَلِيٍّ وَابْنِ مَسْعُودٍ وَابْنِ عُمَرَ وَعِمْرَانَ بْنَ الْحُصَيْنِ أَنَّ الْقَارِنَ يَطُوفَ طَوَافَيْنِ وَيَسْعَى سَعْيَيْنِ، وَلأَنَّ الْقِرَانَ هُوَ الْجَمْعُ وَمَنْ لَمْ يَفْعَلُ إِلَّا أَحَدَهُمَا لَمْ يَكُن جَامِعًا، وَلِأَنَّهُ لَا تَدَاخُلَ فِى الْعِبَادَةِ كَمَا فِي الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ.
There is no intermingling in intended acts of worship. And driving the sacrificial animal after garlanding is similar to talbiyah, because one does that only if intending Hajj or Umrah; thus it became a characteristic like talbiyah. The purpose of talbiyah is to show response to the call, and by garlanding the sacrificial animal showing response is also achieved. Showing response may be by action as it is by speech. This is because the garland is among the rituals of Hajj like talbiyah, so when it is connected to intention, he becomes muhrim like talbiyah. And al-Tahawi and Sa'id ibn Mansur reported from 'Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, and 'Imran ibn al-Husayn that the Qarin performs two tawafs and two sa'ys, and because qiran is combination; whoever does only one of them is not a combiner, and because there is no intermingling in worship as in prayer and fasting.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi principle that in acts of worship intended for themselves (al-ibadat al-maqsuda), intermingling (tadakhul) is not permitted. The author applies this to the Qarin, who performs Hajj and Umrah in one Ihram. The principle is proven through two arguments: first, by analogy to the garlanding (taqlid) of the sacrificial animal, which, like talbiyah, indicates the beginning of Ihram—both are actions that signify the intention to perform Hajj/Umrah, and thus one becomes muhrim by them when combined with intention. Second, through direct narrations from four Companions stating that the Qarin must perform two separate tawafs and two sa'ys. The reasoning is supported by qiyas: just as in prayer and fasting, one cannot combine two distinct acts into one performance (e.g., intending both Zuhr and 'Asr in one prayer), so too Hajj and Umrah, being distinct intended worships, require separate acts. The Hanafi ruling is that the Qarin does two tawafs and two sa'ys.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Intermingling or overlap of one act with another such that a single performance suffices for multiple obligations.
العبادات المقصودة (al-ibadat al-maqsuda): Acts of worship that are intended for their own sake, not merely as means, such as Hajj and Umrah.
قارن (qarin): A person who performs both Hajj and Umrah in a single Ihram without disengaging.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He performs one tawaf and one sa'y for both Hajj and Umrah.
B) He performs two tawafs and two sa'ys, one pair for each.
C) He performs one tawaf and two sa'ys.
D) He is exempted from tawaf and sa'y.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school decisively holds that the Qarin must perform two tawafs and two sa'ys; no intermingling of intended acts is allowed.

Substitutes Are Only Appointed by Shari'ah
States the principle and applies it to the Qarin's substitute fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that substitutes are only valid by divine appointment and apply it to determine when fasting is an acceptable substitute for dam in Hajj al-Qiran.

Core Idea
Substitutes for worship have no validity beyond what the Lawgiver explicitly permits; humans cannot invent them.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi principle: that substitutes (abdal) for acts of worship are not valid unless the Lawgiver has explicitly prescribed them. The passage applies this to a Qarin who cannot afford a sacrifice—fasting is allowed only during Hajj days. If missed, the substitute is void and the original sacrifice becomes obligatory.

Guided Path
1
State the legal scenario
Identify the case: a pilgrim performing Hajj al-Qiran who cannot afford the required sacrifice (dam) is permitted to fast three days as a substitute.
2
Identify the condition
The substitute fasting must be performed before Yawm al-Nahr (the Day of Sacrifice), i.e., during the days of Hajj.
The text confines the substitute to 'the time of Hajj'.
3
Apply the principle
If the fasting is not completed before Yawm al-Nahr, the substitute is invalid because substitutes are only valid as prescribed by Shari'ah.
The principle: 'الأبدال لا تنصب إلا شرعًا'.
4
Conclude the ruling
The original obligation of dam becomes binding (wajib) and fasting after the time is not accepted.
Dam is determined (صار الدم متعينًا).
5
Derive the evidence
The Quranic text (2:196) confines the substitute to the time of Hajj, so it cannot be extended beyond by analogy or personal reasoning.
النَّصُّ حَصَّهُ بِوَقْتِ الْحَجِّ

إِنَّ الْأَبْدَالَ لَا تُنْصَبُ إِلَّا شَرُعًا. إِنْ لَمْ يَصُمُ الثَّلاثَةَ حَتَّى دَخَلَ يَوْمُ النَّحْرِ لَمْ يُجْزِهِ الصَّوْمُ أَصْلَا، وَصَارَ الدَّمُ مُتَعَيِّنًا ، لِأَنَّ الصَّوْمَ بَدَلٌ وَالْأَبْدَالُ لَا تُنصَبُ إِلَّا شَرُعًا، وَالنَّصُّ حَصَّهُ بِوَقْتِ الْحَجِّ وَجَوَازُ الدَّمِ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ.
Indeed, substitutes are not appointed except by Shari'ah. If he does not fast the three [days] until the Day of Nahr enters, fasting does not avail him at all, and the blood [sacrifice] becomes determined, because fasting is a substitute and substitutes are not appointed except by Shari'ah, and the text confined it to the time of Hajj, and the permissibility of blood [sacrifice] remains on the original.

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi principle that substitutes (abdal) for acts of worship are valid only if prescribed by the Lawgiver; humans cannot invent substitutes. The author applies this to the Qarin who must offer a sacrifice (dam) for performing Qiran. If he cannot afford a sacrifice, he may fast three days during Hajj (before Yawm al-Nahr) as a substitute. However, if he delays until after Yawm al-Nahr, fasting is no longer valid because the text (Quran 2:196) confined the substitute fasting to the time of Hajj. The original obligation (dam) becomes binding and cannot be replaced after its time. This ruling illustrates that the validity of substitutes is determined solely by the Shari'ah.

Key Terms
أبدال (abdal): Substitutes for obligatory acts, such as fasting in place of sacrifice, valid only if prescribed by Shari'ah.
بدل (badal): A substitute act that stands in place of an original obligation when the original cannot be performed due to incapacity.
دم (dam): A blood sacrifice (e.g., a sheep) required as an original obligation for the Qarin or for penalty.

Substitutes Are Only Established by Shari'ah
Establishes the principle that substitutes in worship are defined by sacred law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that substitutes are only established by Shari'ah and apply it to determine the ruling for a qarin who misses the fast before the Day of Nahr.

Core Idea
Substitutes in worship are defined by sacred law, not by personal opinion—once the time for a substitute expires, the original obligation returns.

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a critical usul principle that governs the substitution of religious obligations: the Shari'ah alone designates what may substitute for a required act and within what time. The author applies this to the case of a pilgrim performing Hajj al-Qiran who fails to fast three days in lieu of the sacrificial animal before the Day of Nahr. Understanding this principle prevents relying on personal reasoning to extend a substitute beyond its prescribed limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
The passage concerns a qarin (one performing Hajj al-Qiran) who did not fast three days before the Day of Nahr. Determine the ruling: his fasting after that day is invalid, and the dam becomes wajib.
The Day of Nahr is 10 Dhul Hijjah.
2
Extract the underlying principle
The text states: 'al-abdal la tunsabu illa shar'an'—substitutes are only appointed by the Shari'ah. This principle means that no one may create or alter a substitute based on personal opinion.
This is a usul principle rooted in the limits of human reasoning.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The evidence is from Quran 2:196, which confines the substitute fasting to 'fi al-hajj' (during the Hajj). The Hanafi interpretation restricts this to before the Day of Nahr, based on the practice of the Companions and the context of the verse.
The dalil is qat'i in thubut (Quran) but zanni in dalala (interpretation).
4
Recognize the practical consequence
Once the Day of Nahr arrives, the time for the substitute expires. The qarin cannot arbitrarily choose to fast later; the original obligation (dam) becomes due. This reinforces that human reasoning cannot extend a divinely prescribed time limit.
This prevents the common error of assuming fasting can be made up later.

Translation: Substitutes are only appointed by the Shari'ah. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that it is not correct to appoint a substitute for any ruling by one's own opinion; rather, whatever substitute the Shari'ah has appointed for something, that will be it. That is, appointing a substitute is dependent on the Shari'ah. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the qarin (one performing Hajj al-Qiran) did not fast three days in place of the sacrifice until the Day of Nahr arrived, then in this situation a dam (blood sacrifice) becomes due upon him, and he cannot fast now. Because the Shari'ah appointed fasting before the Day of Nahr as a substitute for the dam. Now if he fasts after the Day of Nahr, then in this case it would necessitate appointing a substitute by himself, and appointing a substitute by oneself is not permissible. Therefore, fasting after the Day of Nahr is not correct. And al-Tahawi and Sa'id ibn Mansur narrated from 'Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, and 'Imran ibn al-Husayn that the qarin performs two tawafs and two sa'ys, and because qiran is the combining (of Hajj and 'Umrah), and whoever does not perform both has not combined, and because there is no overlap in worship as in prayer and fasting. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Qiran, vol. 2, p. 42, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Qiran, vol. 1, p. 282, Rahmaniyyah] If he does not fast the three days until the Day of Nahr enters, his fasting is not acceptable at all, and the blood sacrifice becomes determined, because fasting is a substitute, and substitutes are only appointed by the Shari'ah, and the text confined it to the time of Hajj, and the permissibility of the blood sacrifice remains on the original rule. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Qiran, vol. 2, p. 388, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami]
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental usul al-fiqh principle: substitutes (abdal) in acts of worship are solely determined by the sacred law, not by human reasoning. The author then applies it to a specific fiqh issue: a person performing Hajj al-Qiran (combining Hajj and 'Umrah) who failed to fast three days in lieu of the sacrificial animal (dam) before the Day of Nahr (10th Dhul Hijjah) cannot fast after that day. Once the Day of Nahr arrives, the option of fasting is void, and the dam becomes obligatory. The proof from the Qur'anic text confines the alternative fasting to the time of Hajj. The passage then cites a narration from leading Sahaba (Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Umar, Imran ibn Husayn) transmitted by al-Tahawi and Sa'id ibn Mansur, establishing that a qarin must perform two tawafs and two sa'ys because qiran implies combining both rites. This reinforces the principle of non-overlap in worship (no tadakhul). The commentary clarifies that the ruling is based on the Shari'ah's specific designation of the substitute and its time.

Key Terms
بدل (badal): A substitute for a religious obligation, e.g., fasting instead of sacrifice in Hajj.
قارن (qarin): One who performs Hajj and 'Umrah together in a single state of ihram.
دم (dam): Blood sacrifice (usually a sheep or goat) required as compensation for certain omissions in Hajj.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that substitutes are established solely by Shari'ah prevents personal reasoning from extending a prescribed alternative beyond its designated time; once the Day of Nahr arrives, the option of fasting is replaced by the obligation of the dam.

Precedence of the Prohibiting Proof Over Permissibility
Applies the usul principle that when proofs conflict, the one indicating prohibition prevails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of preferring prohibition evidence when proofs conflict and explain the nuanced Hanafi ruling on Isha'ar in Hajj.

Core Idea
When proofs conflict, prohibition prevails over permissibility out of caution.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi usul principle: when a proof of prohibition (muhrim) conflicts with a proof of permissibility (muyassir), the prohibition is given priority. The author of al-Hidayah applies this principle to the case of Isha'ar, the practice of making a small incision on a sacrificial camel or cow to mark it. Understanding this principle helps resolve apparent contradictions between hadiths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
State the principle: when muhrim and muyassir conflict, tarjih is given to muhrim. This is based on ihtiyat (caution).
The principle is general and applies across fiqh.
2
Identify the two conflicting proofs
The muhrim: general hadith prohibiting muthlah (mutilation). The muyassir: authentic hadiths of the Prophet performing Isha'ar during Hajjat al-Wada'.
One is general prohibition, the other is specific prophetic action.
3
Apply the principle to Isha'ar
According to Imam Abu Hanifah, Isha'ar resembles muthlah, so the muhrim is preferred, making Isha'ar makruh.
This is a direct application of the tarjih rule.
4
Resolve the apparent conflict
The commentary offers two reconciliations: (1) the prohibition of muthlah is earlier, the prophetic practice is later, so no actual conflict (naskh or takhsis); (2) Isha'ar is not muthlah (minor wound vs. cutting off limbs).
The conflict is resolved without discarding any proof.
5
Contextualize Imam Abu Hanifah's ruling
Imam Abu Hanifah's makruh ruling was situational due to excessive practice in his time, not an absolute prohibition. Later Hanafi position leans toward permissibility.
This shows the importance of understanding historical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَوْ وَقَعَ التَّعَارُضُ بَيْنَ الْمُحْرِمِ وَالْمُسِيحِ فَالتَّرجِيحُ لِلْمُحْرِمِ. ترجمہ: جب محرم اور میسج کے درمیان تعارض آجائے تو ترجیح محرم کو دی جائے گی۔ تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر دلیل محرم یعنی حرمت ثابت کرنے والی دلیل اور دلیل میسج یعنی اباحت ثابت کرنے والی دلیل کے مابین تعارض آجائے ، یعنی ایک دلیل سے بھی کی حرمت ثابت ہو رہی ہو جبکہ دوسری دلیل سے اسی بھئی کی حلت ثابت ہو رہی ہے تو اس صورت میں حرمت والی دلیل کو ترجیح دی جائے گی یعنی اس بھئی پر حرام کا حکم لگایا جائے گا، اس لئے کہ اس میں احتیاط ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ امام ابوحنیفہ رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک اشعار ( یعنی اونٹ اور گائے کو زخم لگا کر خون نکالنا ) مکروہ ہے، کیونکہ یہ مثلہ ہے اور مثلہ حرام ہے، جبکہ دوسری دلیل سے اشعار کی اباحت ثابت ہو رہی ہے، اب جب محرم اور میسج کے درمیان تعارض واقع ہوا تو محرم کوترجیح دی جائے گی۔ فائدہ: آنحضرت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے مثلہ سے غزوہ خیبر کے وقت منع فرمایا ، جبکہ آپ نے اشعار حجتہ الوداع کے موقع پر کیا، تو اشعار کرنا متاخر ہے اس لئے تعارض نہیں ہے۔ تعارض اس وقت ہوتا جب دونوں باتوں کی تاریخ معلوم نہ ہوتی۔ نیز اشعار مثلہ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ ہر زخم مثلہ نہیں ہوتا بلکہ مثلہ وہ ہوتا ہے کہ جانور کی ناک، کان وغیرہ اعضاء کاٹ دیئے جائیں۔ اشعار میں تو معمولی زخم لگایا جاتا ہے۔ اشعار کا ثبوت صحیح اور صریح احادیث سے ہے، اس لئے اسے مکروہ کہنا درست نہیں۔ امام ابوحنیفہ رحمہ اللہ نے اشعار کو مطلقاً مکروہ نہیں کہا بلکہ آپ کے زمانے میں لوگ اشعار میں بہت مبالغہ کرتے تھے اور یہ خدشہ ہوتا تھا کہ اشعار کی وجہ سے کہیں جانور ہلاک نہ ہو جائے اس لئے آپ نے مکر وہ کہا۔ یہ تو جیہ بہتر ہے تا کہ آپ کی طرف صریح احادیث الهداية كتاب الحج، باب التمتع ج ۱ ص ۲۸۵، ط: رحمانیه
Translation: When there is a conflict between the muhrim (proof of prohibition) and the muyassir (proof of permissibility), the preference is given to the muhrim. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if a conflict occurs between a proof establishing prohibition (muhrim) and a proof establishing permissibility (muyassir), i.e., one proof indicates the prohibition of something while another proof indicates its permissibility, then in that case the proof of prohibition is given preference, i.e., the thing is ruled as forbidden, because there is caution (ihtiyat) in it. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that according to Imam Abu Hanifah, Isha'ar (making a wound on the camel or cow to let blood) is makruh (disliked), because it is Muthlah (mutilation), and Muthlah is haram, while another proof indicates the permissibility of Isha'ar. So when conflict occurred between muhrim and muyassir, preference was given to muhrim. Benefit: The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited Muthlah at the time of the Battle of Khaybar, while he performed Isha'ar on the occasion of his Farewell Pilgrimage (Hajjat al-Wada'), so Isha'ar is later (in time), therefore there is no conflict. Conflict would occur if the dates of both matters were unknown. Also, Isha'ar is not Muthlah, because not every wound is Muthlah; rather, Muthlah is when the animal's nose, ears, etc. are cut off. In Isha'ar, a minor wound is made. The evidence for Isha'ar is from authentic and explicit hadiths, so calling it makruh is not correct. Imam Abu Hanifah did not say Isha'ar is makruh absolutely, but rather in his time people exaggerated in Isha'ar, and there was fear that the animal might die due to it, so he called it makruh. This explanation (tawjih) is better so that explicit hadiths [are not contradicted]. [al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Hajj, Bab al-Tamattu', vol. 1, p. 285, Rahmaniyyah]
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents another usul principle: when two proofs conflict—one indicating prohibition (muhrim) and one indicating permissibility (muyassir)—the prohibiting proof is given precedence due to caution (ihtiyat). The author applies this to the issue of Isha'ar (making a small incision on a sacrificial camel or cow to mark it). According to Imam Abu Hanifah, Isha'ar is makruh because it resembles Muthlah (mutilation), which is haram. However, explicit hadiths show that the Prophet himself performed Isha'ar during his Farewell Pilgrimage. The commentary resolves this apparent conflict in two ways: first, the Prophet's prohibition of Muthlah at Khaybar was earlier, while his Isha'ar was later, so there is no actual conflict (the later action abrogates or specifies the earlier). Second, Isha'ar is not truly Muthlah, as Muthlah involves cutting off parts like the nose or ears, while Isha'ar is just a minor wound. The passage further explains that Imam Abu Hanifah did not declare Isha'ar absolutely makruh; rather, he considered it makruh in his time because people overdid it, risking the animal's life. This nuanced explanation shows that the ruling was situation-specific, not a blanket condemnation.

Key Terms
محرم (muhrim): A proof that indicates prohibition (haram) of an act.
مسیج (muyassir): A proof that indicates permissibility (ibahah) of an act.
اشعار (isha'ar): Making a small incision on the left side of the hump of a sacrificial camel or cow to let blood, as a mark of consecration.
مثلہ (muthlah): Mutilation of an animal by cutting off limbs or parts like nose, ears, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason Imam Abu Hanifah considered Isha'ar makruh?
A) He believed the Prophet never performed Isha'ar.
B) He thought Isha'ar resembled muthlah, which is haram.
C) He considered all wounds on animals to be mutilation.
D) He wanted to discourage Hajj altogether.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method reconciles conflicting proofs through chronology and definition before applying the principle favoring prohibition, and Imam Abu Hanifah's ruling on Isha'ar was contextual, not absolute.

Tarjih of Muharram over Mubih: The Case of Ish'ar
Explains the principle of tarjih and its application to ish'ar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of preferring prohibitory evidence over permissive evidence (tarjih) and apply it to the case of ish'ar, distinguishing between valid and exaggerated practice.

Core Idea
When prohibition and permission conflict, the prohibitory evidence prevails — but only if there is a genuine conflict, as the case of ish'ar shows.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi rule of tarjih: when a prohibitory evidence (muharram) and a permissive evidence (mubih) appear to conflict, the prohibiting evidence is given preference. The rule is applied to the practice of ish'ar (marking the sacrificial animal by incision) during Hajj. At first glance, ish'ar seems permissible (sunnah) yet conflicts with the prohibition of muthlah (mutilation). We will see how the Hanafi commentators resolve this apparent conflict through chronology and definition, clarifying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the rule: when muharram and mubih conflict, which is preferred and why? The rule is based on caution (ihtiyat).
2
Examine the apparent conflict
Identify the two pieces of evidence: the Prophet's sunnah of ish'ar (permissive) and the prohibition of muthlah (prohibitory). Why do they seem to conflict?
3
Resolve the conflict
Use chronology and definition: the prohibition of muthlah came earlier (after Uhud), while ish'ar was practiced later (Farewell Hajj). Also, ish'ar is not muthlah because muthlah involves disfigurement like cutting ears/nose, not a controlled incision.
4
Understand Abu Hanifah's position
Abu Hanifah reportedly disliked ish'ar. How is this reconciled? The commentator explains that his dislike was for the exaggerated practice of his time (deep cutting risking death), not the proper sunnah.
5
Apply the principle
What is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) on ish'ar today? Proper ish'ar is sunnah; exaggerated ish'ar is makruh tanzihi.

وَلَوْ وَقَعَ التَّعَارُضُ بَيْنَ الْمُحْرِمِ وَالْمُبِيحِ فَالتَّرْجِيحُ لِلْمُحْرِمِ. لَمَّا وَقَعَ التَّعَارُضُ بَيْنَ كَوْنِ الْإِشْعَارِ سُنَّةً وَبَيْنَ كَوْنِهِ مُثْلَةً وَهِيَ حَرَامٌ فَالرُّجْحَانُ لِلْحَرَامِ، لِأَنَّ الْمُحَرِّمَ مَعَ الْمُبِيحِ إِذَا اجْتَمَعَا فَالْمُحَرِّمُ أَوْلَى. وَعِنْدِي إِطْلَاقُ اسْمِ الْمُثْلَةِ عَلَى الْإِشْعَارِ مُشْكِلٌ، لِأَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ نَهَى عَنِ الْمُثْلَةِ فِي أَوَّلِ مَقْدَمِهِ إِلَى الْمَدِينَةِ، وَأَشْعَرَ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْهَدَايَا فِي آخِرِ حَيَاتِهِ عَامَ حَجَّةِ الْوَدَاعِ. فَلَوْ كَانَ الْإِشْعَارُ مِنْ بَابِ الْمُثْلَةِ لَمَا أَشْعَرَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، لِأَنَّهُ نَهَى عَنْهَا قَبْلَ ذَلِكَ. وَالْكَلَامُ الصَّحِيحُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: إِنَّ أَبَا حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ كَرِهَ الْإِشْعَارَ الْمُحْدَثَ الَّذِي يُفْعَلُ عَلَى وَجْهِ الْمُبَالَغَةِ، وَيُخَافُ مِنْهُ السِّرَايَةُ إِلَى الْمَوْتِ لَا مُطْلَقَ الْإِشْعَارِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: وَلَوْ وَقَعَ التَّعَارُضُ فَالتَّرْجِيحُ لِلْمُحَرَّمِ قَدْ يُقَالُ: لَا تَعَارُضَ فَإِنَّ النُّهَي عَنْهُ كَانَ بِأَثَرِ قِصَّةِ الْعُرَنِيِّينَ عَقِيبَ غَزْوَةِ أُحُدٍ وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ الْإِشْعَارَ كَانَ بَعْدَهُ، فَعُلِمَ أَنَّهُ إِمَّا مَخْصُوصٌ مِنْ نَصِّ نَسْخِ الْمُثْلَةِ مَا كَانَ هَدْيًا، أَوْ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِمُثْلَةٍ أَصْلًا، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ، إِذْ لَيْسَ كُلُّ جُرْحٍ مُثْلَةً، بَلْ هُوَ مَا يَكُونُ تَشْوِيهًا كَقَطْعِ الْأَنْفِ وَالْأُذُنَيْنِ وَسَمْلِ الْعُيُونِ، فَلَا يُقَالُ لِكُلِّ مَنْ جُرِحَ مُثْلَ بِهِ. وَالْأَوْلَى مَا حَمَلَ عَلَيْهِ الطَّحَاوِيُّ مِنْ أَنَّ أَبَا حَنِيفَةَ إِنَّمَا كَرِهَ إِشْعَارَ أَهْلِ زَمَانِهِ، لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يَهْتَدُونَ إِلَى إِحْسَانِهِ وَهُوَ شَقُّ مُجَرَّدِ الْجِلْدِ لِيُدْمِيَ، بَلْ يُبَالِغُونَ فِي اللَّحْمِ حَتَّى يَكْثُرَ الْأَلَمُ وَيُخَافُ مِنْهُ السِّرَايَةُ.
And if conflict occurs between the prohibiting and the permitting, then preference is given to the prohibiting. Because when conflict occurred between ish'ar being a sunnah and it being a muthlah (which is haram), preference was given to the haram, because when the prohibiting and the permitting come together, the prohibiting is more appropriate. In my view, applying the term muthlah to ish'ar is problematic, because the Prophet ﷺ forbade muthlah at the beginning of his arrival in Madinah, and he ﷺ performed ish'ar on the sacrificial animals at the end of his life in the year of the Farewell Pilgrimage. If ish'ar were a form of muthlah, the Messenger of Allah ﷺ would not have done it, because he forbade it before that. The correct statement in this chapter is that Abu Hanifah (may Allah have mercy on him) disliked the innovated ish'ar that is done excessively and from which harm leading to death is feared, not ish'ar in absolute terms. And his statement: 'And if conflict occurs, then preference is given to the prohibiting' may be objected to: there is no conflict, because the prohibition of muthlah occurred after the story of the 'Urayniyyīn following the Battle of Uhud, and it is known that ish'ar was after that. So it is known that it is either an exception from the text abrogating muthlah for what was a sacrificial animal, or that it is not muthlah at all, which is the truth. For not every wound is muthlah; rather, muthlah is that which disfigures, like cutting the nose, ears, and gouging out eyes. So it is not said of everyone who is wounded that he is muthlah. And the best interpretation is what al-Tahawi carried it on: that Abu Hanifah only disliked the ish'ar of the people of his time, because they do not know how to do it properly (splitting only the skin to cause bleeding), but they exaggerate in the flesh until pain increases and harm leading to death is feared.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the principle of tarjih (preference) when evidence for prohibition (muharram) conflicts with evidence for permissibility (mubih). The rule is that the prohibiting evidence is given preference because it is more cautious. The author applies this to the issue of ish'ar (making a small incision on the hump of a sacrificial camel or cow to mark it) during Hajj. The apparent conflict: ish'ar is reported as a sunnah practice of the Prophet ﷺ, yet the Prophet also forbade muthlah (mutilation of animals). The principle would give precedence to the prohibition, making ish'ar disliked. However, the commentator clarifies that there is no real conflict. Hadith chronology: the prohibition of muthlah came earlier (after the incident of the 'Urayniyyīn following Uhud), while the Prophet's ish'ar occurred later during the Farewell Pilgrimage. Thus, either the prohibition was specific to non-sacrificial animals, or ish'ar is not considered muthlah. The correct view is that ish'ar is not muthlah because muthlah involves disfigurement like cutting ears, nose, or gouging eyes, whereas ish'ar is a controlled incision. As for Imam Abu Hanifah's reported dislike of ish'ar, it is interpreted (following al-Tahawi) as referring to the exaggerated practice of his contemporaries who cut deeply into the flesh causing pain and risking the animal's life, not the proper practice as done by the Prophet. This resolves the apparent contradiction between Imam Abu Hanifah's statement and authentic hadiths.

Key Terms
التعارض (al-ta'āruḍ): The apparent conflict between two pieces of evidence that seemingly lead to opposite rulings on the same issue.
المحرم (al-muḥarrim): A piece of evidence that indicates prohibition (tahrīm) of an act.
المبيح (al-mubīḥ): A piece of evidence that indicates permissibility (ibāḥah) of an act.
الإشعار (al-ish'ār): The practice of making a small incision on the hump of a sacrificial camel or cow to mark it as a hady (sacrificial animal) during Hajj.
المثلة (al-muthlah): Mutilation of a living creature by cutting off limbs or disfiguring features; forbidden in Islam.
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): Giving preference to one evidence over another when they conflict, based on established criteria.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary, why did Imam Abu Hanifah consider ish'ar (marking the sacrificial animal by incision) as disliked (makruh)?
A) Because ish'ar is a form of muthlah (mutilation) which is forbidden.
B) Because the people of his time performed ish'ar excessively, cutting deep into the flesh, risking the animal's life.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ never performed ish'ar himself.
D) Because ish'ar contradicts the principle of kindness to animals (rahmah).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of preferring prohibitory evidence over permissive evidence does not automatically render ish'ar prohibited; through chronological and definitional analysis, the correct sunnah practice is upheld, while harmful exaggeration is condemned.

Ish'ar and Muthlah: Resolving the Conflict
Resolving conflict between ish'ar and muthlah via specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that ish'ar is permissible and excessive ish'ar is makruh tanzihi, and evaluate the opposing claim that ish'ar is muthlah.

Core Idea
Ish'ar is not muthlah; the Prophet's practice after the prohibition proves it.

This passage addresses an apparent conflict between two evidences: the prohibition of muthlah (disfigurement) and the Prophet's practice of ish'ar (marking sacrificial animals). Some argued that ish'ar falls under muthlah, hence impermissible. The Hanafi response demonstrates that ish'ar is fundamentally different from muthlah, and only excessive, dangerous cutting is disliked. Understanding this reconciliation is key to applying Hanafi methodology in resolving conflicting proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Apparent Conflict
Recognize that ish'ar is considered sunnah by some but muthlah is haram. This creates a prima facie contradiction between two pieces of evidence: one permissive and one prohibitive.
The principle that when prohibitive and permissive evidence conflict, the prohibitive takes precedence is acknowledged but then shown to be inapplicable.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Refutation
Note the chronological order: the Prophet prohibited muthlah upon arrival in Medina, and later performed ish'ar during the Farewell Pilgrimage. If ish'ar were muthlah, he would not have done it after the prohibition.
This argument uses the Prophet's actual practice (fi'l) to specify the scope of the prohibition.
3
Define Muthlah Precisely
Muthlah is not any wound; it is specifically disfigurement like cutting off the nose, ears, or gouging eyes. A simple cut for marking is not muthlah.
This linguistic precision prevents overextension of the prohibition.
4
Understand Abu Hanifa's Dislike
Abu Hanifa disliked ish'ar only when done excessively—cutting deep into the flesh, causing severe pain and risk of death. Proper ish'ar (superficial cut) is not disliked.
Al-Tahawi explains that the dislike was for the ignorant practice of later times, not for ish'ar itself.
5
Apply the Principle of Conflict Resolution
Since there is no actual conflict (ish'ar is not muthlah), the principle that the prohibitive evidence takes precedence does not apply. The permissive evidence stands.
The Hanafi method reconciles the proofs by specifying the scope of each.

لَمَّا وَقَعَ التَّعَارُضُ بَيْنَ كَونِ الْإِشْعَارِ سُنَّةٌ وَبَيْنَ كَوْنِهِ مُثْلَةً، وَهِيَ حَرَامٌ فَالرُّجُحَانُ لِلْحَرَامِ، لِأَنَّ الْمُحَرِّمَ مَعَ الْمُبِيحِ إِذَا اجْتَمَعَا فَالْمُحَرِّمُ أَوْلَى، وَعِنْدِي إِطْلَاقُ اسْمِ الْمُثْلَةِ عَلَى الْإِشْعَارِ مُشكِلٌ، لأنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ نَهَى عَنْ الْمُثْلَةِ فِي أَوَّلِ مَقْدَمِهِ إِلَى الْمَدِينَةِ وَأَشْعَرَ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْهَدَايَا فِي آخِرِ حَيَاتِهِ عَامَ حَجَّةِ الْوَدَاعِ فَلَوْ كَانَ الْإِشْعَارُ مِنْ بَابِ الْمُثْلَةِ لَمَا أَشْعَرَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ لِأَنَّهُ نَهَى عَنْهَا قَبْلَ ذَلِكَ، وَالْكَلَامُ الصَّحِيحُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: إِنَّ أَبَا حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ كَرِهَ الْإِشْعَارَ الْمُحْدَتَ الَّذِي يُفْعَلُ عَلَى وَجْهِ الْمُبَالَغَةِ، وَيُخَافُ مِنْهُ السِّرَايَةُ إِلَى الْمَوْتِ لَا مُطْلَقُ الْإِشْعَارِ. حاشية الشلبى على تبيين الحقائق كتاب الحج، باب التمتع ج ۲ ص ۴۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي (قَوْلُهُ : وَلَوْ وَقَعَ التَّعَارُضُ فَالتَّرْجِيحُ لِلْمُحَرَّمِ قَدْ يُقَالُ: لَا تَعَارُضَ فَإِنَّ النُّهَى عَنْهُ كَانَ بِأَثَرِ قِصَّةِ الْعُرَنِينَ عَقِيبَ غَزْوَةِ أَحَدٍ وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ الْإِشْعَارَ كَانَ بَعْدَهُ، فَعُلِمَ أَنَّهُ إِمَّا مَخْصُوصٌ مِنْ نَصٌ نَسْحَ الْمُثْلَةِ مَا كَانَ هَدْيَا أَوْ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِمُثْلَةٍ أَصْلَا، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ ، إِذْ لَيْسَ كُلُّ جُرْحٍ مُثْلَةٌ بَلْ هُوَ مَا يَكُونُ تَشْوِيهَا كَقَطْعِ الْأَنْفِ وَالْأُذُنَيْنِ وَسَمْلِ الْعُيُونِ، فَلَا يُقَالُ لِكُلِّ مَنْ جُرِحَ مُثْلَ بِهِ، وَالْأَوْلَى مَا حَمَلَ عَلَيْهِ الطَّحَاوِيُّ مِنْ أَنَّ أَبَا حَنِيفَةَ إِنَّمَا كَرِهَ إِشْعَارَ أَهْلِ زَمَانِهِ، لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يَهْتَدُونَ إِلَى إِحْسَانِهِ وَهُوَ شَقُّ مُجَرَّدِ الْجِلْدِ لِيُدْمِيَ، بَلْ يُبَالِغُونَ فِي اللَّحْمِ حَتَّى يَكْثُرَ الْأَلَمُ وَيُخَافُ مِنْهُ السِّرَايَةُ. فتح القدير: كتاب الحج، باب التمتع ، ج ۳ ص ۹، ط: دار الفكر
When there occurred a conflict between ish'ar being sunnah and it being muthlah (disfigurement), and muthlah is haram, then the preponderance is for haram, because when the prohibitive and the permissive come together, the prohibitive takes precedence. In my view, applying the term muthlah to ish'ar is problematic, because the Prophet prohibited muthlah at the beginning of his arrival in Medina, and he performed ish'ar on sacrificial animals at the end of his life in the year of the Farewell Pilgrimage. So if ish'ar were from the category of muthlah, the Prophet would not have done it after prohibiting it. The correct statement in this regard is to say that Abu Hanifa disliked the innovated ish'ar that is done excessively, where there is fear of it leading to death, not absolute ish'ar. (Hashiyat al-Shalabi) ... It may be said: There is no conflict, for the prohibition was after the incident of the 'Uraniyyin after the battle of Uhud, and it is known that ish'ar came after that. So it is known that it is either specifically excluded from the text of the prohibition of muthlah for what is a sacrificial animal, or that it is not muthlah at all, which is correct, because not every wound is muthlah; rather it is what disfigures, like cutting off the nose and ears and gouging out eyes. So it is not said that everyone who is wounded is muthlah. The best is what al-Tahawi carried it upon: that Abu Hanifa only disliked the ish'ar of the people of his time because they did not know how to do it properly—it is merely cutting the skin to cause bleeding, but they go deep into the flesh so that pain increases and death is feared. (Fath al-Qadir)
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage addresses the apparent conflict between two evidences: (1) the hadith prohibiting muthlah (disfigurement), and (2) the Prophet's practice of ish'ar (marking sacrificial animals by making a cut). Some claimed that ish'ar is a form of muthlah, hence makruh or haram. The passage refutes this by showing that the Prophet himself performed ish'ar after prohibiting muthlah, proving that ish'ar is not muthlah. The author of Hashiyat al-Shalabi clarifies that Abu Hanifa's dislike (karāhah) was only for excessive ish'ar that cuts deep into the flesh, risking death. Al-Tahawi likewise explains that Abu Hanifa disapproved of the ignorant practice of his time. Thus, the principle is that when a prohibitive and a permissive evidence conflict, the prohibitive takes precedence, but here there is no actual conflict because ish'ar is not muthlah. The discussion shows the Hanafi method of reconciling seemingly contradictory proofs by specifying the scope of the disliked practice.

Key Terms
إشعار (ish'ār): Marking a sacrificial animal by making a small cut on its hump to indicate it is a hady.
مثلة (muthlah): Disfigurement or mutilation of a living being, prohibited in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that ish'ar is not muthlah?
A) Because the Prophet prohibited muthlah after ish'ar.
B) Because the Prophet performed ish'ar after prohibiting muthlah, showing ish'ar is not muthlah.
C) Because Abu Hanifa said so.
D) Because muthlah only applies to animals, not humans.

Key Takeaway
Ish'ar is permissible and not muthlah; only excessive, dangerous ish'ar is disliked.

Completeness of Jinaayah with Completeness of Benefit
States principle that crime completes with complete benefit, applied to perfume.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that jinaayah is complete with complete benefit to determine whether a dam or sadaqah is due for applying perfume in ihram.

Core Idea
The completeness of a muhrim's crime depends on the completeness of the benefit derived.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi principle from the chapter on crimes (jinaayat) during hajj. The author of Al-Hidayah uses a simple case—applying perfume—to illustrate how the extent of benefit (irtifaaq) determines the penalty. Understanding this 'illa helps you predict rulings in similar scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the question
The act is a muhrim applying perfume (or similar substances) to his body. The question is: what penalty is due?
2
Locate the principle
The text states: 'Indeed, the crime becomes complete with the completeness of benefit.' This is the governing principle.
3
Apply the principle to the case
If the perfume is applied to a complete limb, the benefit is complete, so the crime is complete and a dam (sheep) is wajib. If applied to less than a limb, the benefit is partial, so the crime is deficient and only sadaqah (charity) is wajib.
4
Consider the underlying reasoning
The Hanafi school uses the effective cause (illa) of complete benefit to derive the ruling. This is a clear example of qiyas jaliy.

Source Text

باب الجنايات
(۲۱۱) إِنَّ الْجِنَايَةَ تَتَكَامَلُ بِتَكَامُلِ الْإِرْتِفَاقِ .
ترجمہ: انتفاع کے مکمل ہونے سے جرم بھی کامل ہو جاتا ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جنایت کامل ہوتی ہے کامل نفع اٹھانے سے، یعنی محرم جب کامل فائدہ اٹھائے گا تو جنایت بھی کامل ہو گی ، تو دم لازم آئے گا اور جب فائدہ کامل نہ اٹھائے تو جنایت ناقص ہوگی اور ناقص جنایت کی وجہ سے کفارہ لازم آئے گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ محرم نے اگر مکمل عضو پر خوشبو لگائی یا اس سے زیادہ حصے پر تو دم لازم آئے گا، کیونکہ مکمل عضو پر خوشبولگانا یہ کامل فائدہ اٹھانا ہے اور کامل فائدہ سے جنایت بھی کامل ہوتی ہے اور جنایت جب کامل ہوتی ہے تو دم آتا ہے، لہذا اس شخص پر بھی دم آئے گا۔ اور اگر عضو سے کم میں خوشبو لگائی تو کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔
الهداية كتاب الحج، باب الجنايات ج ۱ ص ۲۸۸، ط: رحمانیه
tَتَجِبُ شَاةٌ إِنْ طَيِّبَ مُحْرِمٌ عُضْوا، وَإِلَّا تَصَدَّقَ أَوْ خَصْبَ رَأْسَهُ بِحِنَّاءِ أَوْ أَدْهَنَ بِزَيْتٍ لِأَنَّ الْجِنَايَةَ تَتَكَامَلُ بِتَكَامُلِ الارْتِفَاقِ وَذَلِكَ فِي الْعُضْوِ الْكَامِلِ فَيَتَرَتَّبُ عَلَيْهِ كَمَالُ الْمُوجِبِ وَتَتَقَاصَرُ الْجِنَايَةُ فِيمَا دُونَهُ فَوَجَبَتْ الصَّدَقَةُ.
البحر الرائق: كتاب الحج، باب الجنايات ، ج ۳ ص ۲، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
Chapter of Crimes
(211) Indeed, the crime becomes complete with the completeness of benefit.
Translation: The crime becomes complete with the completeness of benefit.
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that the crime becomes complete with taking complete benefit, meaning when the muhrim takes complete benefit, the crime will also be complete, so a dam becomes obligatory, and when he does not take complete benefit, the crime will be deficient and due to the deficient crime, kaffarah becomes obligatory.
The author of Hidayah mentions the mentioned principle and states that if the muhrim applies perfume to a complete limb or more than that, then a dam becomes obligatory because applying perfume to a complete limb is taking complete benefit, and with complete benefit the crime also becomes complete, and when the crime is complete, dam comes, so also upon this person comes dam. And if he applies perfume to less than a limb, then kaffarah becomes obligatory.
Al-Hidayah: Book of Hajj, Chapter of Crimes, vol. 1 p. 288, ed. Rahmaniyyah
"A sheep becomes obligatory if a muhrim perfumes a limb, otherwise he gives charity, or he dyes his head with henna or anoints with oil, because the crime becomes complete with the completeness of benefit, and that is in the complete limb, so based on it the completeness of the obligator is established, and the crime is reduced in what is less than that, so charity becomes obligatory."
Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: Book of Hajj, Chapter of Crimes, vol. 3 p. 2, ed. Dar al-Kitab al-Islami]
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle from the Hanafi school regarding violations (jinaayat) committed by a person in the state of ihram (muhrim). The principle states that the completeness of a prohibited act is determined by the completeness of the benefit (irtifaaq) derived from it. The author applies this to the case of a muhrim applying perfume (khushbu) to his body. If he applies it to a complete limb (like the entire arm) or more, the benefit is considered complete, so the crime is complete and a dam (a sheep sacrifice) is obligatory. If he applies it to less than a limb (e.g., just part of the arm), the benefit is partial, so the crime is deficient and only sadaqah (charity) is required. This illustrates the Hanafi methodology of using 'illa (effective cause) to determine rulings. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, as found in Al-Hidayah. The mufta bihi position is that dam is obligatory for application to a complete limb. The key is to understand that the ruling varies based on the extent of benefit, not merely the act itself.

Key Terms
جنایت (jinaayah): A prohibited act committed by a muhrim, such as applying perfume, shaving, hunting, etc.
ارتفاق (irtifaaq): The benefit or enjoyment derived from an action. Here, the criterion for whether a crime is complete or deficient.
دم (dam): A sheep or goat that must be sacrificed as a penalty for a complete crime in ihram.
صدقہ (sadaqah): Charity given as a penalty for a deficient crime, equivalent to the value of half a sa' of wheat or similar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in this passage, what is the penalty if a muhrim applies perfume to an entire arm (a complete limb)?
A) A sheep (dam) and charity (sadaqah)
B) Only charity (sadaqah)
C) A sheep (dam) only
D) No penalty because he had no sinful intent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the penalty for applying perfume in ihram based on the completeness of benefit: a complete limb requires dam, less than a limb requires sadaqah.

Sleep and Coercion: Sin Removed, Ruling Remains
States that sin is lifted by sleep/coercion but ruling remains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on sleep/coercion and apply it to the case of a muhrim whose head is shaved without consent, identifying the penalty (dam) and its cause.

Core Idea
Sleep and coercion lift sin but not liability: benefit triggers the ruling of dam even without intent.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between moral sin (ithm) and worldly legal liability (hukm). A person may be excused from sin due to sleep or coercion, but if they derive benefit from an act, the associated ruling—such as dam (sacrifice) for a muhrim—still applies. This lesson explores this maxim through the case of a muhrim whose head is shaved without his permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Read the Arabic maxim and understand its plain meaning: 'Due to sleep and coercion, sin is removed without the ruling.'
The maxim separates moral accountability from legal consequences.
2
Locate the specific case
The passage applies the maxim to a muhrim whose head is shaved while asleep or under coercion. Identify the two parties: the one shaved and the one who shaves.
Both are without sin, but their liabilities differ.
3
Determine the ruling on each party
The one shaved owes a dam (wajib), and the one who shaves owes sadaqah (charity). State these rulings explicitly.
The dam is on the muhrim despite lack of consent.
4
Understand the cause (sabab)
The reason dam is obligatory is the benefit (raahah, zeenah) the muhrim gains from the shaving. This cause exists regardless of his state.
The shaver's sadaqah is due to his active role without permission.
5
Distinguish sin from ruling
Sin (ma'tham) is removed because the muhrim had no control, but the worldly ruling (dam) remains because the benefit is real.
This is a foundational Hanafi principle in acts related to Hajj.

وعندنا بسبب النوم والإكراه ينتفى المأثم دون الحكم يعنى ينتفى الإثم الذي هو حكم الآخرة دون الحكم الذى يتعلق بالدماء وقد تقرر سببه أى سبب وجوب الفدية والواو فيه للحال(وهو) أى السبب ما نال من الراحة والزينة أى ما نال المحلوق من الزينة والراحة بزوال الشعث ومن الزينة بزوال انتشار الشعر.
البناية كتاب الحج، باب الجنايات، ج ۴ ص ۳۳۸، ط: دار الكتب العلمية ]
الهداية: كتاب الحج، باب الجنايات فصل، ج ۱ ص ۳۰۳، ط: رحمانیه
(212) Indeed, due to sleep and coercion, the sin is removed without the ruling.
Translation: Due to sleep and coercion, sin is removed, not the ruling.
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that due to sleep and coercion (forcing someone), from the sleeping and coerced person, sin is removed but the ruling will not be removed, meaning the ruling will be established. From the perspective of the Hereafter, he will not be sinful, but worldly accountability in the form of dam will occur.
The author of Hidayah mentions the mentioned principle and states that if someone shaves the head of a muhrim without his permission, i.e., he was asleep or he was forcibly shaved, then in both cases upon the shaver is sadaqah, and upon the one whose head was shaved is dam. The reason is that due to shaving without the permission of the created being in such a manner that he was asleep or forcibly shaved, the sin is removed, meaning the shaved person will not be sinful from the hereafter perspective, but from the worldly perspective the ruling is established and upon him dam becomes obligatory, even though this shaving occurred without his permission, still dam becomes obligatory because the cause of the obligation of dam is the benefit of relief, and this cause exists in the case of the sleeping and coerced person.
"And according to us, due to sleep and coercion, sin is removed without the ruling, meaning the sin which is the ruling of the Hereafter is removed without the ruling that relates to blood; and its cause has been established, i.e., the cause of the obligation of the ransom, and the waw is for the state (and it is) i.e., the cause is what he obtains of relief and adornment, i.e., what the shaved person obtains of adornment and relief by the removal of dishevelment, and from adornment by the removal of scattered hair."
Al-Binayah: Book of Hajj, Chapter of Crimes, vol. 4 p. 338, ed. Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah]
Al-Hidayah: Book of Hajj, Chapter of Crimes, section, vol. 1 p. 303, ed. Rahmaniyyah
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi legal maxim that sleep (nawm) and coercion (ikraah) remove the sin (ithm) but do not remove the legal ruling (hukm) in worldly matters. The specific application is: if a muhrim's head is shaved without his consent—while he is asleep or under duress—the person whose head is shaved still owes a dam (sacrifice), even though he is not sinful in the afterlife. The reason is that the cause (sabab) for the dam is the benefit of relief and adornment (irtifaaq al-raahah wa al-zeenah) that he experiences from the shaving, which is present regardless of his state. The one who performs the shaving, however, owes sadaqah (charity) for his action. This shows that legal liability in the Hanafi school can sometimes be separated from moral culpability. The passage quotes from Al-Binayah and Al-Hidayah to confirm this as the school's position. The mufta bihi ruling is that dam is obligatory on the muhrim whose head is shaved without his consent, as long as he obtains benefit (relief) from it.

Key Terms
نوم (nawm): Sleep; a state that removes legal responsibility for sin but not for the worldly ruling due to benefit.
اکراہ (ikraah): Coercion or compulsion applied to someone against their will; similar to sleep in effect on sin.
مأثم (ma'tham): Sin; the moral wrong that incurs punishment in the Hereafter.
حکم (hukm): The legal ruling applicable in this world (e.g., obligation of dam), which remains despite removal of sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
